{}, "cyoa_variable_effects": {}, "cyoa_question_swaps": {}, "cyoa_answer_swaps": {}, "banner_data": {} } ] function getTooltips(str) { let matches = []; tooltipList.forEach((tooltip, index) => { // Adjust the regex to match searchString potentially surrounded by “ and followed by optional punctuation let regex = new RegExp(`(?<=\\b|\\s|^|“)${tooltip.searchString}(?=[.,;!?]?\\b|\\s|”|$)`, 'g'); let match; while ((match = regex.exec(str)) !== null) { matches.push({ start: match.index + (match[0].startsWith('“') ? 1 : 0), // Adjust for potential starting “ end: match.index + match[0].length - (match[0].endsWith('”') ? 1 : 0) - (match[2] ? 1 : 0), tooltipIndex: index }); } }); // Sort by starting position; if two start at the same position, longer match comes first matches.sort((a, b) => a.start - b.start || b.end - b.start - (a.end - a.start)); // Filter out overlaps for (let i = 0; i < matches.length - 1;) { if (matches[i + 1].start < matches[i].end) { matches.splice(i + 1, 1); // Remove the next match since it overlaps } else { i++; // Move to next match } } return matches; } function applyTooltips(str) { const matches = getTooltips(str); let result = ''; let lastIndex = 0; matches.forEach(match => { const tooltip = tooltipList[match.tooltipIndex]; result += str.slice(lastIndex, match.start); result += `${tooltip.searchString}${tooltip.explanationText}`; lastIndex = match.end; }); result += str.slice(lastIndex); // Add the remainder of the original string return result; } function applyTooltipsToObject(obj) { for (let key in obj) { if (typeof obj[key] === 'string') { obj[key] = applyTooltips(obj[key]); } else if (typeof obj[key] === 'object') { applyTooltipsToObject(obj[key]); // Recursive call } } } The Day of Decision

" e.pages = ["

In spite of all that has assailed you throughout this term, the chance for electoral redemption arrives all the same. Yet with how you’ve found your life broken and battered from the pains of life inside and out of governance, you find it a struggle to care. Sure, you may wind up triumphant at night’s end, but will the average American you’ve desperately tried to sell your vision of hope to feel a sense of satisfaction in four more years of Hubert Humphrey?

Irrespective of your personal hangups, the night begins conspicuously enough. Minnesota and Massachusetts are called for you as polls close, while Tower easily routs the Deep South. As the industrial Midwest hangs in the balance, though, that same feeling of dread encompasses you much as it did in 1968. You embark on the first of many walks through the White House terrace, bringing Senator Mondale along with you.

This man shares the same wish for a better tomorrow as you do, and has been beaten down by a political world that cannot understand his longing as you once were. But Hubert Humphrey has risen to the top of this world through appealing to its cruel acolytes, and could you bear to see Walter throw away his bravery for the sake of electoral success? If you haven’t paved a path for him to carry his brand of politics, that may be what he is forced to do. Before you can come up with an answer to the questions, you’re back at the doors to the Situation Room.

The picture is far more optimistic this time around. Those union strongholds are swinging your way, and it’s not too long before 270 is just out of reach. As the stress drains from your staffers bodies and the major news stations claim your victory, you can’t stand to watch it any longer. Stepping out to the Rose Garden, no one stands there to congratulate you or laud your strength.

It's a win, sure, but it is merely numbers in an arbitrary system. It says little about how you’ve done as President; that is for you and America to decide. As you look back, you see a nation which has been enriched by the charity of a government its very people gave power to, a return of citizens from the hell of the war to the safety of family and friends. These achievements could be considered the bare minimum for a country’s success, yet they have still evaded the United States for years. You brought these accomplishments to fruition, yet you get the feeling you will not go down as one of America’s most revered heads of state. This fact does not particularly bother you, however. Your life is coming to a close far sooner than you’d hoped, and even if you tried as hard as you could, you get the sense that your grand vision could not be finished alone. Men like Walter Mondale, Fred Harris, and chief of all Edmund Muskie are there to fill in those gaps and bring their own influence to government. You already explained your plan to turn over the reins to the Vice President earlier tonight in the event of your reelection; this new dawn is already in motion. But today, that brave mayor of Minneapolis turned stern Senator is not George Washington, Abraham Lincoln, or Franklin Roosevelt. What Hubert Horatio Humphrey has done, is doing, and will continue to do for America, is enough.

The Resignation-January 21st, 1973

I’ll keep this short, but I would like to begin this address by again expressing my gratitude for being reelected to a second term in the White House. There is perhaps no greater feeling than having millions of Americans entrust you with the most dangerous and difficult job on the planet today. However, I cannot continue in the office of President of the United States in a sound capacity. A few short weeks ago I was briefed on the presence of an aggressive form of cancer in the bladder. Doctors have informed me that it is terminal, and the chance of recovery is minimal. I became aware of this in the final days of my campaign, hence my absence from the public sphere during that time. I did not bring this up during my reelection efforts because I did not wish to inhibit either side of the political spectrum from expressing their candid thoughts on my presidency, and I would have gotten far too many sympathy cards(crowd laughs). I will return to my beloved Minnesota to spend my final time in the company of dear family and friends, and I shall tender my resignation from the Presidency effective at midnight tomorrow. With that in mind, I am passing down the responsibilities of the presidency to my closest colleague, Mr. Edmund Muskie. From the first day I saw him, I knew this man was fit for any job he wanted, and I hope that I have left him a model vision of America to build upon. To my political supporters, please do not be concerned by these developments: Ed shall continue on the platform of a clean world, a fair world, and a charitable world that I fought for over this campaign. To my constituents across the globe, but most of all those from Minnesota, thank you for giving a beat down kid born in a drugstore not just a shot in politics, but a shot at life.

"] e.executable.push([0, (() => {e.image = e.image = 'https://file.garden/aPv9uE9PkXWKuNYV/1972BAMHumphreyGoodEndingTest.jpg'})]) e.executable.push([1, (() => {e.image = e.image = 'https://file.garden/aPv9uE9PkXWKuNYV/BAMHumphreyResigns.jpg'})]) if (SongChange === 1) { const justAnotherPresEnding = new Playlist(); ctsAchievement("Controlled Demolition"); ctsAchievement("Final Curtain"); const justAnotherPresSong = new Song( "Everyday People", "Sly and the Family Stone", "https://file.garden/aPv9uE9PkXWKuNYV/71L08HPfvyL._UF1000%2C1000_QL80_.jpg", "https://audio.jukehost.co.uk/tGpndwSe8y4oWKet7HXEYQGpxHnQYTxN" ); justAnotherPresEnding.addSong(justAnotherPresSong); SongChange = 1; changePlaylist(justAnotherPresEnding); } return construct(0); } if (DomesticAgenda < 4 && VietnamWar < 5) { e.header = "

Hawaii (United States of America)

" e.pages = ["

Hawaii, officially the United States of America (USA), is a country in the Pacific Ocean about 2,000 miles (3,200 km) southwest of the American Commonwealth. It has a total area of 28,311 square kilometres (10,931 square miles), with 58.8% of it being composed of land, and the remaining 41.2% water. The combined territories under USA control consist of 137 volcanic islands that comprise the entire Hawaiian archipelago. The largest metropolitan area is that of New Washington (the capital), formerly Honolulu, which is located on the island of Washburn, formerly Oʻahu. With around 3.5 million inhabitants, Hawaii is the most densely populated country in North America.

Settled by Polynesians sometime between 1000 and 1200 CE, Hawaii was home to numerous independent chiefdoms. In 1778, British explorer James Cook was the first known non-Polynesian to arrive at the archipelago. An influx of European and American explorers, traders, and whalers soon arrived, leading to the decimation of the once-isolated indigenous community through the introduction of diseases such as syphilis, tuberculosis, smallpox, and measles; the native Hawaiian population declined from between 300,000 and one million to less than 40,000 by 1890. Hawaii became a unified, internationally recognized kingdom in 1810, remaining independent until American and European businessmen overthrew the monarchy in 1893; this led to annexation by the U.S. in 1898. The Second American Revolution (1936-1945) resulted in the loss of the American mainland to Socialist forces and the flight of the USA central government to Hawaii. The victorious Socialists proceeded to establish the American Commonwealth under Provisional Chairwoman Elizabeth Gurley Flynn, and took the USA’s position as the dominant geopolitical power in North America.

Though fleeing to the island as a nominally fascist one-party state, the death of President Richard Washburn Child in 1946 saw the USA slowly transition back into a liberal democracy over the course of three decades, with democratically elected presidents being reinstated in 1976. Historians largely attribute this slow change in policy to a mix of numerous factors, including the capitulation of the Kingdom of Italy in 1945, diplomatic pressure from the British Empire, and the governmental purges by President Douglas MacArthur (1946-1964). The first democratically elected President of the USA post-second revolution was Nelson Rockefeller, standing for the American Liberty League.

The political status of the USA is contentious. The USA maintains its claim of being the sole legitimate government of America, and it regards the American Commonwealth as an illegal entity. In turn, the American Commonwealth also denies the legitimacy of the USA, claiming it occupies territory that belongs to an indigenous republic. Though formerly recognised by both the British and Japanese empires, the normalization of relations between the American Commonwealth and Britain in the 70s led to them recognising the Commonwealth instead, whilst the collapse of the Japanese Empire in 1991 deprived the USA of their final major international backer. Very few nations currently recognise the USA as a country, with the most notable being the State of Mexico."] e.image = "https://file.garden/aI9Kl6CdkxABOmSP/1936%20MOW/Endings/MOW%20Ending%2014%20Image.png"; if (SongChange != 1) { const flynnEnding = new Playlist(); ctsAchievement("Taiwan-ification"); const flynnSong = new Song( "Hold the Fort", "Pete Seeger, The Almanac Singers", "https://file.garden/aI9Kl6CdkxABOmSP/1936%20MOW/Album%20Covers/MOW%20Almanac%20Singers.png", "https://audio.jukehost.co.uk/5Ak7TOMLD2OakpDbm3IuK01MLaWhRSTF" ); flynnEnding.addSong(flynnSong); SongChange = 1; changePlaylist(flynnEnding); } return construct(0); } if (Fate > 16 && LegionLeader === 2) { //Special Formatting corrr = '\n

SUNDAY, JANUARY 26TH, 1947

On Wednesday the 22nd, at 20:08 HST, the Japanese Akizuki-class destroyer Niizuki was sunk eight miles off the coast of Kauai after being struck by a barrage of rounds shot from Hawaiian coastal artillery. The ship, which, according to Japanese officials, had been patrolling the area to protect Japanese merchant vessels following numerous skirmishes between the two countries over their pacific claims, entered the US’s territorial waters without their express permission, resulting in a brief shootout. SUNDAY, SEPTMEBER 4TH, 1949

Most people remember where they were when they heard that George Moseley was captured. My wife was with her militia, garrisoning a newly-occupied Columbus. My brother was back up in Chicago, helping sort rations in our neighbourhood’s distribution center. As for me, I was down in Louisiana, watching in person as my comrades apprehended the tyrant.

It was a sunny day in New Orleans, the clear sky giving an incongruous tone to the fierce battle waging below. The Red Guard detachment I was commanding had just managed to dislodge the last few Legionnaires holding out in the city’s port, disarming the meagre number who surrendered and reappropriating their defenses in case of a counter-attack. Overall, the assault on the fascists’ temporary capital had been going better for us than expected. The other captains and I had gloomily expected it to turn into one long, drawn-out siege, but, much to our (at the time unknown) luck, part of the Generalissimo’s inner circle had rebelled against him, depriving his remaining troops of half a command structure. It seemed that, from the moment it had overthrown its predecessor, to its very last fight, Moseley’s regime was destined to cannibalise itself.

Exhausted from lugging around sandbags, I slumped down against the wall of a nearby warehouse, pulling out my pocket-sized edition of Malatesta’s At The Café for entertainment. Yet, I had made it through no more than three sentences before the crack of rifle fire sent me running towards the embankment. The militiamen perched at the edge of the harbor had spotted a small boat sailing southwards down the Mississippi, a small group of shoddily cloaked figures aboard. The lack of a red or white flag had immediately heightened my comrades’ suspicions, and, when the vessel had refused their orders to stop, they opened fire with the intention to sink. Bullets swiftly peppered the hull, sending the craft beneath the waves and its passengers scrambling towards the shore. Unfortunately for them, the only path back to dry land was via our position.

One by one, the failed escapees were pulled ashore, their sodden disguises torn off to reveal none other than the American Mussolini and his personal guard. At first, my compatriots and I were in disbelief - there was a higher chance we had captured an unfortunate lookalike than the man himself - but the medals, the entourage, they were confirmation enough. I scrambled over the rubble to find our radio officer, shaking her by the shoulders as I ordered her to relay the news. The machine whirred to life, four words transmitting throughout the city: “We have the President.”

Slowly, the bursts of gunfire and artillery that had permeated the day’s events went quiet, bathing New Orleans in a ghostly silence. Then, a cheer. From the streets, the harbors and the field hospitals, people cried out in joy and relief. Three years of hell were finally over. Tears trickled down the faces of even my most aloof comrades as they held each other and discussed what they would do when they returned home to their loved ones. He wasn’t in charge anymore, and he never would be again.

The Convention-Meeting the Moment

In a true drag out fight for the nomination perhaps not seen since the 1924 Convention, dark horse Georgia Governor James Earl Carter narrowly triumphed over the forefather of the eponymous DNC reform Commission and South Dakota senator George McGovern through the critical support of moderate delegates at the midnight hour.

Carter’s unlikely rise to the forefront of the Democratic field was the brainchild of political wild child Hamilton Jordan and the strategy now termed “Momentum” by analysts, which saw a crowded field of likely nominees and influential southern figures whittled down as the grassroots Carter campaign built up momentum as a middle of the road alternative to a growing intra-party divide. McGovern, for his part, focused largely on “guerilla warfare” techniques which preyed on Carter’s weaknesses in more urban, liberal areas that were no longer beholden to party bosses as they were back in 1968. As the candidates headed to the Milwaukee convention, Carter held a slim majority built up from dominating victories in the South as well as close shaves in key primary states such as Maryland and New Jersey. McGovern, for his part, was aided by a smattering of clear Northeastern and Plains victories including New York and New Hampshire, as well as control of California and Nevada’s winner take all delegation by left wing Governor Jerry Brown. However, McGovern’s early vow to select a vice president at the convention as a show of preparation for campaigning fell through as his first four choices for the position all turned him down, greatly weakening his perceived sway in the wider Democratic fold. Though Brown instructed his delegates to pledge to McGovern, Carter would be buttressed by key endorsements from George Wallace and Henry Jackson, coalescing the last remaining moderates into Carter’s camp as last minute uncommitted defections put the peanut farmer over the line. Still, McGovern was offered and accepted the position of vice president, hoping to unify the party enough to avoid a left wing insurgency in his name. Despite the wide ideological spectrum that the ticket covers, both men hail from primarily agricultural, sparsely populated states, bringing into question the strength of their appeal in the typical Democratic working class boroughs.

", "

The Indefinable

As though nothing has changed, Hubert Humphrey awakes to begin another day. However, the more time spent in this body ravaged by its own cancerous devices, the more conscious the former President becomes of his own mortality. Today, such awareness grows into something deeper as he takes another look in the bedside mirror with eyes sunken and face fallen; The Happy Warrior must consider how it is to be remembered. Simply for the body of work he has dedicated his life to, his name and the names of his many colleagues will forever take up the margins of American history books, their lives and decisions judged by the whim of the writer’s pen. Many will simply recall him as nothing more than a footnote, with even more giving him no regard whatsoever. But what about those people who will remember?

Will they be the sons and daughters of the men sent off to Vietnam, forever resentful of what he and Lyndon subjugated their fathers to? Or will they be the young ones who were given the tools necessary for success by programs like the Civil Rights Act and HEAD Start? In time, will it simply end up being the scholars, who dedicated their lives to the impersonal study of hundreds of men in the mold of him? Hubert Humphrey has not always been in control of his life while he has been alive to see it, so how could he possibly control what people do with it in his absence?

Some may view him through his championing of those living in the shadows, from the young to the elderly and the sickly. Others will look at his reputation as a conciliatory warmonger, one who prioritized political points and preservation of the status quo over true progress. But Humphrey is neither of those things in isolation, nor both in excess. He is what is lost somewhere in the middle of the endless debates over legacy and shortcoming. What truly molds him were the equal moments of humanity and inhumanity that drove decision making, the strife and emotion shown only behind closed doors, the pain from failure unable to be shown for fear of weakness. Ultimately, Hubert Horatio Humphrey is but a man.

Vladimir Karelin, Soviet Commissar of Foreign Affairs, peered out over the balcony and across the skyline of Washington D.C., his lips pursing in a combination of distaste and disappointment. This was supposed to be the hub of American democracy? He had seen pictures, of course, but he had expected something more… flashy? Something more in-tune with the wasteful lifestyle of the western bourgeoisie, but, instead, he was met with neatly organised rows of office buildings that failed to hide masses of sprawling slums. Well, at least the city was indicative of the country at large.

The sound of tailored shoes on sandstone prompted him to swivel on the spot, away from the urban view and towards the door that had just opened. Out stepped a round-faced man with thin, greying hair and a kind smile - the very one Vladimir had seen plastered over the front page of every foreign newspaper last November. “President France,” the diplomat began, holding out his hand, “it’s an honor to finally meet you.”

Joseph Irwin France let out a humble chuckle, taking the socialist’s hand and shaking it firmly, “The honor is all mine, Mister Karelin. I’ve been looking forward to this meeting ever since they got around to inaugurating me. It’s a shame it didn’t happen fifteen years ago.”

Vladimir nodded, his eyes narrowing behind his glasses ever so slightly as he looked the President up and down. He had met many capitalist leaders before, and was used to them viewing the Republics of Labour as a mere tool to be used and discarded. As a result, he had become accustomed to the typical red flags present in western diplomats; sleaze, infantilization, and, more often than not, blatant racism. Yet, the man before him - the man who represented a global superpower so diametrically opposed to everything he and his comrades stood for - seemed nothing other than completely genuine, if not a little nervous.

“Is everything alright, Mister Karelin?” President France queried, a faint unease to his words.

Vladimir cleared his throat. “Oh, yes, sorry. My mind wandered for a second,” he straightened himself up, placing a hand on the balcony as he once again turned towards the skyline, “I agree, this meeting has been a long time coming. None of your successors would’ve been open-minded enough to ever consider something like this, especially Mister Child.”

An unfamiliar fury flashed across the President’s face. “The less said about that man the better. He will get his due this term, even if it destroys any chance at my reelection.”

“A capitalist of principle,” the corners of Karelin’s lips twitched upwards, betraying his stoic demeanor, “You’re a rare breed, Mister President. Now, I assume you didn’t invite me all the way here just to bestow recognition on our humble Republics. What do you want?”

Joseph sighed, deflated by the ambassador’s focus on business. “Well, as you could probably tell, the US is struggling at the moment, especially with food poverty. The agricultural surplus we had at the beginning of the Depression has practically vanished, what with the Dust Bowl and farms going bankrupt. My administration wishes to create a program to help feed poor families, but we’re worried there isn’t enough food to go around-”

“So you want some from us.”

“You’re a nation of farmers,” an apologetic smile crossed France’s face, as if he were embarrassed to be asking for anything to begin with, “if any country could give us grain and still have more than enough left over, it’s the Soviets.”

Karelin raised his eyebrow skeptically. “And what do we get in return?”

The President’s eyes darted around the balcony, before he leant in and lowered his voice. “Arms, Mister Karelin. I’m not blind to the situation in Europe. I know Britain and Italy are sizing you up, readying to pounce at any moment. In its current state, the Red Guard has no chance against either of their militaries, let alone the two combined.”

The ambassador froze. The poster child of modern capitalism supplying weapons to the largest socialist nation in the world? It was a baffling suggestion. Comical, even. And yet, was it one the revolution could afford to pass up? He was right - the Red Guard was able to protect its own territory, but a continent-wide war would see them stretched disastrously thin. Sensing Karelin’s uncertainty, France continued: “For all our differences, Vladimir, I’d rather see the old world under the red flag than the iron fist of fascism,” his eyes softened, laying bare an anxiety the socialist was all too familiar with, “We almost had our own Mussolini over here. Trust me when I tell you I want to help prevent that from happening anywhere else.”

Vladimir clicked his tongue as he mulled over his few remaining objections. None of them were more convincing than the guilt that settled in his stomach at the thought of a Europe subjected by that hateful ideology. He held out his hand once more. “Okay, Joseph. We have a deal.”"] e.image = "https://file.garden/aI9Kl6CdkxABOmSP/1936%20MOW/Endings/MOW%20Ending%209%20Image.png"; if (SongChange != 1) { const franceEnding = new Playlist(); ctsAchievement("C’est la vie"); const franceSong = new Song( "A Room With A View", "Artie Shaw", "https://file.garden/aI9Kl6CdkxABOmSP/1936%20MOW/Album%20Covers/MOW%20Room%20With%20A%20View.png", "https://audio.jukehost.co.uk/NSILETREXOyT0WdSCbEnYTBEsuagBn0G" ); franceEnding.addSong(franceSong); SongChange = 1; changePlaylist(franceEnding); } return construct(0); } } if (aa[0].candidate === 118 && aa[0].electoral_votes > 265) { //Democrat Victory Endings if (PartyComposition === 3) { e.header = "

Back to Normal

" e.pages = ["

A calming wind blew through the streets of Chicago, ruffling George’s neatly combed hair as he lounged in his seat, taking in the ambiance of the riverside cafe. From across the table, his business associate and close friend, Maxwell, fiddled with a box of expensive cigars, his fingers fumbling with the ornate latch holding it closed. It was only by the time their coffees had arrived that he finally pried it open, causing their cups to rattle slightly as he revealed the contents with a relieved “hmph”. Wordlessly, George reached over the table, plucking a smoke from its container and rolling it between his fingers, his eyes lighting up at the familiar sensation of the wrapper against his skin.

Click. Click. Two cigar caps fell into the ashtray, discarded. From his pocket, Maxwell produced a lighter - silver plated and engraved - and held it out to his friend. George placed the rolled tobacco in his mouth, leaned forward, and let the flame alight its foot. The first inhale he took made the worries of the material world slip away. It was over. After all this time, it was finally over.

Maxwell sighed out a cloud of smoke, grinning at his compatriot’s exaggerated bliss. “Back to normal?”

“Back to normal, indeed,” George replied breathlessly, a smile tugging at the corner of his lips. “I was worried there for a second, you know? What with the amount of election material the Liberty League was bandying around, I had almost resigned myself to four more years of this barbarity. Thank the Lord the results came in when they did. A couple hours longer and I would’ve poured all my champagne down the sink.“

A hearty chuckle bubbled up from within Maxwell’s chest. “Oh, please, Georgie, you can’t fool me. You’d sooner die than waste a good bottle. Still, I can’t deny everything tastes much sweeter with victory,” he took a sip of the coffee to illustrate his point, grimacing as the bitter flavour coated his tongue. “To think we were this close to becoming a cheap imitation of Italy… A hundred and fifty years of American democracy, nearly torn down by a man no one had heard of ten years ago. Imagine what the founding fathers would think.”

George’s eye flicked upwards at the invocation of the founders, surveying the cloudless heavens with a proud expression. “Whatever they did, I bet they’re smiling down on us now, knowing that the electorate has kept their vision alive,” he tapped his finger on the table in emphasis. “This nation has its hiccups, but it will always be a bastion of democracy, liberty and equali- Oop! To your left, Max.”

Maxwell gracefully shifted in his seat, just in time for a brick to fly past his head and crash into the empty table behind them, sending the whole thing careening to the floor.

Unamused, he turned his gaze to the origin of the projectile: a congregation of enraged dockworkers on the opposing embankment, hemmed in by an equally ill-tempered line of policemen. Picket signs demanding higher wages and improved conditions lay strewn across the cobblestone, scuffed and broken from the officers’ boots as they steadily kettled the demonstrators.

“God, can’t those layabouts see that we’re trying to have a conversation?” Max scoffed, getting to his feet to wave a fist at the crowd. In his frustration, his cigar slipped out of his mouth, rolling off the side of the cafe terrace and into the water with a comical plop. “Get back to work, you bottom-feeders! We don’t pay you to complain all day!” Shaking his head, he slumped back onto the chair like a frustrated father. “Honestly, people these days. No respect.”

“Now, now, don’t try to rile them up. They’re like hungry dogs, they’ll snap at anything they see. Let the officers do their jobs,” George pushed his friend’s coffee back towards him, attempting to return the atmosphere to its previous tranquility. Across the river, the police lines hardened. “Anyway, where was I? Ah, yes, democracy, liberty and equality. That’s what defines this country. And that’s why, in hindsight, I don’t believe Child could’ve ever won.”

Taking another cigar from its box, Maxwell nodded. “Mhm. Yes. I concur. He and his lot just didn’t line up with the national ethos. They could talk a big game about patriotism and foreign policy, but, ultimately, they were just too brash for government. Politics is a civil sport - it’s about discussion, consensus, the marketplace of ideas. There is no place for violence within a liberal democracy.” A striker screamed as he was ripped from the group, before being silenced with the sickening crunch of baton against bone.

“And all that government intervention nonsense, too. I know there were a few good apples within his administration who had sensible economics, but the man himself was just obsessed with that corporatism swill,” George paused momentarily to take a swig of his own drink, the break in conversation being filled by the sound of a melee breaking out over the water. “I don’t understand how it’s still a discussion at this point. We’ve seen for decades how the free market brings progress and prosperity to all, yet some lollygaggers persist in advocating for welfare and public utilities and, god forbid, socialism. Why can’t the poor realise capitalism is just in their best interests?” There was a splash as two cops hurled a dockworker into the river. George shuffled backwards in his seat to avoid getting his shoes wet.

“Thankfully, it’s not up to them. It’s up to our new President, who I, for one, have complete trust in.”

George shook his cigar in Maxwell’s direction in a vague but passionate gesture of support. “I couldn’t agree more. There are few men who could guard our sacred system better than Cactus Jack, especially after all this… faff,” he raised his coffee cup aloft with an air of triumph, indicating with his free hand that his partner do the same. “Here’s to America, and the principles that guide it.”

The sound of motors filled the air as a fleet of trucks raced toward the chaos engulfing the dockyard across from them. National guardsmen, rifles primed, descended on the strikers in their dozens. Maxwell just smiled and pressed his cup to George’s.

On the Job

" e.pages = ["

One long, shrill note rang out over the construction site, signaling the beginning of a union-mandated lunch break. Placing his tools safely on the floor, Marco joined the small gaggle of his fellow CWA workers as they ambled towards their de facto hangout spot on the side of the road. The foreman was already there, pulling open the bed of his truck and handing out packed lunches to the frazzled employees. Marco took his with a fatigued “thank you”, before lumbering over to the shade of a nearby silver maple and collapsing onto the ground next to his friend.

“Fuck…” Marco sank back against the bark of the tree, lazily unwrapping his free meal, “Y’know, Benny, I haven't worked this hard since ‘28. And, back then, I was building ships up in New York. Almost lost a couple fingers in the process, but at least they didn’t have me building houses out in the midday sun.”

“Tell me about it,” Benny waved his sandwich around dramatically, “I'm thinking of voting Republican next election just so I can go back to doing nothing.”

Marco chortled through a mouthful of baloney and white bread, crumbs tumbling to the grass below. Swallowing his bite, he opened his mouth to quip back at his friend, when he was interrupted by the arrival of another coworker, Ellis, nose-deep in an unfamiliar newspaper. “Whatcha reading there?”

Ellis peered up from the periodical with a slight pout. “Some rag they were selling in town,” he held out the paper, showing the other two the front cover, “Apparently the President is planning on breaking up the Legion. Got a legal team, a senate committee, the works.”

“Good riddance,” Marco commented, squinting in an attempt to read the first couple lines, “bastards have been begging for a beat down for a while now. You can’t overthrow the government and act like nothing ever happened,” he emitted a resigned sigh, shaking his head “I just hope Hull’s got the balls to actually go through with it.”

Benny merely shrugged, beginning his assault on his second sandwich, “Eh, he’s got us this far-”

Ellis abruptly pulled back the newspaper, his face twisting into a bitter scowl, “Hey, watch your mouth! There ain’t nothing wrong with the Legion. Coxey was a damn traitor, the March on Washington was the best thing that happened to this country.”

For a second, Marco seemed surprised by his colleague’s outburst, only to roll his eyes and return to his trademark cynicism, “Blah blah. Spare me the Union Plot crap. The whole thing was made up.”

“Made up?!” Ellis galloped forward, towering over his seated coworker, “Our country is under attack from fuckin’ ruskies and pinkos and you think that’s all made up?! I didn’t spend two years in the Legion, fighting for your freedom, just for you to spew garbage like-”

Marco’s eye snapped up to Ellis, his mellow grin vanishing, “You what?”

“I said, I didn’t spend two years in the Legion for you to-”

Marco pounced from his position under the tree, tackling Ellis by the waist and knocking him to the ground. Benny could only watch in shock as his normally docile comrade proceeded to straddle the former Legionnaire and land blow after blow on his face, the audible crunching of bone causing heads to turn. The other workers rushed over to the action, forming a circle around the two to better observe the one-sided beat down, some even calling out encouragement to Marco.

“Hey, knuckleheads!” Marco froze mid-punch at the sound of the foreman’s voice, the ring of spectators falling silent as the portly man stepped in and yanked him off of Ellis, “Knock it off! You are government employees, what the hell do you think you’re doing?!”

“He attacked me, sir. He just attacked me outta nowhere!” Stammered Ellis, fresh bruises blossoming on his pale skin.

The foreman’s jaw tightened and he shot daggers at the instigating subordinate, “Is this true, Marco?”

“Not for no reason, sir,” Marco mumbled, his eyes glued to the ground like a scolded child , “He said he was in the Legion.”

There was a short pause, “He did?” The foreman looked to Benny for confirmation, receiving a nervous nod. He took a deep breath, flashing an appalled sneer in Ellis’ direction before patting Marco on the back and lowering his voice, “I’m gonna stand over there for a couple minutes.
